<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="journal_id" content="41477"/>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Genomes of early-diverging streptophyte algae shed light on plant terrestrialization"/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Plants 2019 6:2"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2019-12-16"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.language" content="En"/>

    <meta name="dc.copyright" content="2019 The Author(s)."/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Mounting evidence suggests that terrestrialization of plants started in streptophyte green algae, favoured by their dual existence in freshwater and subaerial/terrestrial environments. Here, we present the genomes of Mesostigma viride and Chlorokybus atmophyticus, two sister taxa in the earliest-diverging clade of streptophyte algae dwelling in freshwater and subaerial/terrestrial environments, respectively. We provide evidence that the common ancestor of M. viride and C. atmophyticus (and thus of streptophytes) had already developed traits associated with a subaerial/terrestrial environment, such as embryophyte-type photorespiration, canonical plant phytochrome, several phytohormones and transcription factors involved in responses to environmental stresses, and evolution of cellulose synthase and cellulose synthase-like genes characteristic of embryophytes. Both genomes differed markedly in genome size and structure, and in gene family composition, revealing their dynamic nature, presumably in response to adaptations to their contrasting environments. The ancestor of M. viride possibly lost several genomic traits associated with a subaerial/terrestrial environment following transition to a freshwater habitat. A study sequenced and analysed two genomes of basal streptophyte algae that adapt to freshwater and subaerial/terrestrial environments, respectively, providing insights into plant terrestrialization, an important evolutionary transition in the history of life."/>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Mounting evidence suggests that terrestrialization of plants started in streptophyte green algae, favoured by their dual existence in freshwater and subaerial/terrestrial environments. Here, we present the genomes of <i>Mesostigma viride</i> and <i>Chlorokybus atmophyticus</i>, two sister taxa in the earliest-diverging clade of streptophyte algae dwelling in freshwater and subaerial/terrestrial environments, respectively. We provide evidence that the common ancestor of <i>M. viride</i> and <i>C. atmophyticus</i> (and thus of streptophytes) had already developed traits associated with a subaerial/terrestrial environment, such as embryophyte-type photorespiration, canonical plant phytochrome, several phytohormones and transcription factors involved in responses to environmental stresses, and evolution of cellulose synthase and cellulose synthase-like genes characteristic of embryophytes. Both genomes differed markedly in genome size and structure, and in gene family composition, revealing their dynamic nature, presumably in response to adaptations to their contrasting environments. The ancestor of <i>M. viride</i> possibly lost several genomic traits associated with a subaerial/terrestrial environment following transition to a freshwater habitat.</p></div></div></section>

                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Transition to a terrestrial environment, termed terrestrialization, is generally regarded as a pivotal event in the evolution and diversification of land plant flora<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Kenrick, P. &amp; Crane, P. The origin and early evolution of plants on land. Nature 389, 33–39 (1997)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d57113e810">1</a></sup>. Extant green plants (Viridiplantae) can be subdivided into two lineages, Chlorophyta (most of the green algae) and Streptophyta (embryophytes and their closest algal relatives, a grade collectively known as streptophyte algae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Becker, B. &amp; Marin, B. Streptophyte algae and the origin of embryophytes. Ann. Bot. 103, 999–1004 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d57113e814">2</a></sup>). There is now compelling evidence that adaptation to subaerial/terrestrial habitats is a feature of streptophyte algae, arising from their dual existence in freshwater and subaerial/terrestrial environments throughout their evolutionary history. Recent transcriptomic and genomic studies have shown that the molecular toolkit for life in a terrestrial environment was already present in streptophyte algae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Delwiche, C. F. &amp; Cooper, E. D. The evolutionary origin of a terrestrial flora. Curr. Biol. 25, R899–R910 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d57113e818">3</a></sup>. Homologues of genes once thought to be restricted to embryophytes are now being detected in streptophyte algae. Examples include those involved in symbiotic or pathogenic interactions with soil microbes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Delaux, P.-M. et al. Algal ancestor of land plants was preadapted for symbiosis. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 112, 13390–13395 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d57113e822">4</a></sup>, phytohormone signalling<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Bowman, J. L. et al. Insights into land plant evolution garnered from the Marchantia polymorpha genome. Cell 171, 287–304 (2017)." href="#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d57113e826">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Hori, K. et al. Klebsormidium flaccidum genome reveals primary factors for plant terrestrial adaptation. Nat. Commun. 5, 3978 (2014)." href="#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d57113e826_1">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Nishiyama, T. et al. The Chara genome: secondary complexity and implications for plant terrestrialization. Cell 174, 448–464 (2018)." href="#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d57113e826_2">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Bowman, J. L., Briginshaw, L. N., Fisher, T. J. &amp; Flores-Sandoval, E. Something ancient and something neofunctionalized—evolution of land plant hormone signaling pathways. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 47, 64–72 (2019)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d57113e829">8</a></sup>, desiccation/stress<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="de Vries, J., Curtis, B. A., Gould, S. B. &amp; Archibald, J. M. Embryophyte stress signaling evolved in the algal progenitors of land plants. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 115, E3471–E3480 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d57113e834">9</a></sup>, plastid/nucleus retrograde signalling<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="de Vries, J., Stanton, A., Archibald, J. M. &amp; Gould, S. B. Streptophyte terrestrialization in light of plastid evolution. Trends Plant Sci. 21, 467–476 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d57113e838">10</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Zhao, C. et al. Evolution of chloroplast retrograde signaling facilitates green plant adaptation to land. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 116, 5015–5020 (2019)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d57113e841">11</a></sup> and cell wall metabolism<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Mikkelsen, M. D. et al. Evidence for land plant cell wall biosynthetic mechanisms in charophyte green algae. Ann. Bot. 114, 1217–1236 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d57113e845">12</a></sup>. Importantly, many transcription factors (TFs) thought to be specific to embryophytes originated in streptophyte algae and also substantially expanded there<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Wilhelmsson, P. K. I., Mühlich, C., Ullrich, K. K. &amp; Rensing, S. A. Comprehensive genome-wide classification reveals that many plant-specific transcription factors evolved in streptophyte algae. Genome Biol. Evol. 9, 3384–3397 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d57113e849">13</a></sup>. These findings raise exciting questions about the functional role of embryophyte-like genes in streptophyte algae.</p><p>In phylogenomic analyses, the earliest-diverging streptophyte algae are represented by a clade comprising two monospecific genera, <i>Mesostigma</i> and <i>Chlorokybus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Lemieux, C., Otis, C. &amp; Turmel, M. A clade uniting the green algae Mesostigma viride and Chlorokybus atmophyticus represents the deepest branch of the streptophyta in chloroplast genome-based phylogenies. BMC Biol. 5, 2 (2007)." href="#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d57113e861">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Timme, R. E., Bachvaroff, T. R. &amp; Delwiche, C. F. Broad phylogenomic sampling and the sister lineage of land plants. PLoS ONE 7, e29696 (2012)." href="#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d57113e861_1">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Wickett, N. J. et al. Phylotranscriptomic analysis of the origin and early diversification of land plants. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 111, E4859–E4868 (2014)." href="#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d57113e861_2">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Wodniok, S. et al. Origin of land plants: do conjugating green algae hold the key? BMC Evol. Biol. 11, 104 (2011)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d57113e864">17</a></sup>. Both are structurally simple but differ in their cellular organization, life history and type of habitat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Cook, M. E. &amp; Graham, L. E. in Handbook of the Protists (eds Archibald, J. M. et al.) 1–20 (Springer International Publishing, 2016)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d57113e868">18</a></sup>. <i>Mesostigma viride</i> is a scale-covered flagellate with an eyespot that reproduces by binary division at the flagellate stage (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#Fig1">1b</a>). <i>Chlorokybus atmophyticus</i> consists of sarcinoid cell packets where each cell has its own cell wall, occasionally producing biflagellate, scaly zoospores (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#Fig1">1b</a>). Importantly, <i>M. viride</i> is found in the benthos of small, shallow ponds whereas <i>C. atmophyticus</i> is a subaerial/terrestrial alga that occurs among bryophytes, on soil and on stones.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Comparative genome profile of M. viride and C. atmophyticus."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: Comparative genome profile of <i>M. viride</i> and <i>C. atmophyticus</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-019-0560-3/MediaObjects/41477_2019_560_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-019-0560-3/MediaObjects/41477_2019_560_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="1031" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p><b>a</b>, This phylogenetic tree was constructed by maximum likelihood based on the concatenated sequences of single-copy genes, while species-specific gene duplicates were excluded from the analysis. A <i>k</i>-means clustering of gene families based on the gene abundance of each species is shown in the right-hand panel; each column represents a family and each row represents one species. <b>b</b>, Differential interference contrast micrographs showing <i>M. viride</i> (left) and <i>C. atmophyticus</i> (right). <b>c</b>, Venn diagrams showing the number of gene families shared among <i>M. viride</i>, <i>C. atmophyticus</i> and a representative rhodophyte, streptophyte or chlorophyte. <b>d</b>, Significant increases and decreases in gene families; filled red circles, triangles and rhombi denote function enrichment of significant increased gene families in the KEGG pathway, while empty symbols denote function enrichment of significant decreased gene families in the KEGG pathway. Details of these functions are shown in the right-hand panel. <b>e</b>, Percentages of total proteins found in both algae and embryophytes (red), proteins shared among algae (purple) and proteins shared among embryophytes (green) based on the classification given in Orthofinder. <b>f</b>, Principal component analysis of the type and number of Pfam domains.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Previous analyses of two streptophyte algal genomes, <i>Klebsormidium nitens</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Hori, K. et al. Klebsormidium flaccidum genome reveals primary factors for plant terrestrial adaptation. Nat. Commun. 5, 3978 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d57113e945">6</a></sup> and <i>Chara braunii</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Nishiyama, T. et al. The Chara genome: secondary complexity and implications for plant terrestrialization. Cell 174, 448–464 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d57113e951">7</a></sup>, have not only revealed embryophyte-like genomic traits with gains and expansions of respective genes/gene families in both taxa, but also loss of genes involved in responses to abiotic stresses that prevail in a terrestrial environment, in the aquatic (freshwater) <i>C. braunii</i> (<i>K. nitens</i> thrives in a subaerial/terrestrial environment). The draft genomes of <i>M. viride</i> and <i>C. atmophyticus</i> reported here allowed us to address two questions important for plant terrestrialization: (1) did the common ancestor of streptophytes already display embryophyte-like genomic traits that would be indicative for adaptation to a terrestrial environment; and (2) how do the genomes of <i>M. viride</i> and <i>C. atmophyticus</i> differ from each other in light of previous genome studies on two streptophyte algae that occur in contrasting environments (subaerial/terrestrial and aquatic) but belong to different streptophyte classes (Klebsormidiophyceae and Charophyceae)?</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome sequencing, genome characteristics and phylogenetic analysis</h3><p>A total of 245 Gb (746.86X) (<i>M. viride</i>) and 66 Gb (775.68X) (<i>C. atmophyticus</i>) raw data were generated using Illumina technology (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#MOESM3">1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#MOESM3">2</a>). Based on <i>k</i>-mer depth distribution analyses, the nuclear genome sizes of <i>M. viride</i> and <i>C. atmophyticus</i> were estimated to be 329 and 85 Mb, respectively (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#MOESM3">3</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#MOESM3">4</a> and Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#MOESM1">1</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#MOESM1">2</a>). Approximately 85.4% (281 Mb) and 87.0% (74 Mb) of the genomes were de novo assembled, consisting of 6,924 scaffolds with a minimum contig length needed to cover 50% of the genome (<i>N</i><sub>50</sub>) of 113,221 base pairs (bp) for <i>M. viride</i>, and 3,836 scaffolds with <i>N</i><sub>50</sub> of 752,385 bp for <i>C. atmophyticus</i> (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#MOESM3">5</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#MOESM3">6</a>). The distributions of genomic copy content (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#MOESM1">3</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#MOESM1">4</a>) and BUSCO score (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#MOESM3">7</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#MOESM3">8</a>) suggested no contamination and good assembly quality. Moreover, 93.8% (<i>M. viride</i>) and 99.4% (<i>C. atmophyticus</i>) of de novo assembled transcripts were aligned to the assembled genome. (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#MOESM3">9</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#MOESM3">10</a>). Both genomes have substantial repeat components (38.7 and 31.3%, respectively; Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#MOESM3">11</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#MOESM3">12</a>). By combining homologue-based, ab initio and transcriptome-based approaches, 9,300 gene models were predicted for both genomes (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#MOESM3">13</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#MOESM3">14</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#MOESM1">5</a>). In total, 9,198 and 9,066 predicted coding sequences, respectively, were supported by sequenced transcripts, indicating the high accuracy of gene predictions (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#MOESM3">15</a>). Complete coverage of genes involved in transcription, translation and DNA synthesis was also obtained (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#MOESM3">16</a>).</p><p>A phylogenetic analysis of 16 genomes from Rhodophyta, Glaucophyta, Chlorophyta, streptophyte algae and embryophytes, based on a concatenated amino acid sequence alignment of 375 orthologues of single-copy genes, confirmed the sister relationship between <i>Mesostigma</i> and <i>Chlorokybus</i> and also supported previous reports that <i>M. viride</i> and C<i>. atmophyticus</i> constitute the earliest-diverging lineage of extant streptophytes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Timme, R. E., Bachvaroff, T. R. &amp; Delwiche, C. F. Broad phylogenomic sampling and the sister lineage of land plants. PLoS ONE 7, e29696 (2012)." href="#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d57113e1104">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Wickett, N. J. et al. Phylotranscriptomic analysis of the origin and early diversification of land plants. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 111, E4859–E4868 (2014)." href="#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d57113e1104_1">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Wodniok, S. et al. Origin of land plants: do conjugating green algae hold the key? BMC Evol. Biol. 11, 104 (2011)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d57113e1107">17</a></sup> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#Fig1">1a</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Comparative genomics</h3><p><i>Mesostigma viride</i>, <i>C. atmophyticus</i> and <i>K. nitens</i> shared 3,683 gene families (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#Fig1">1c</a>), which comprised 4,423 genes in <i>M. viride and</i> 4,210 in <i>C. atmophyticus</i>. Interestingly, 3,639 and 2,470 gene families were found exclusive to <i>M. viride</i> and <i>C. atmophyticus</i>, respectively (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#Fig6">1</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#MOESM3">17</a>). We also considered homologues of the identified genes in the red alga <i>Cyanidioschyzon merolae</i>, and in <i>Micromonas commoda</i> (Chlorophyta). <i>M. viride</i> and <i>C. atmophyticus</i> share about 2,000–2,500 gene families with <i>C. merolae and</i> 3,300–4,100 with <i>M. commoda</i>, reflecting their phylogenetic relationships (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#Fig1">1c</a>).</p><p>To explore significant increases or decreases in gene families in Rhodophyta, Chlorophyta and streptophyte algae, the gene family content of five representative genomes in each of the three clades (a total of 15 genomes) was compared. The most significant increases in gene families occurred from Rhodophyta to streptophyte algae (225) and to Chlorophyta (188), respectively, and referred mainly to plant hormone signal transduction, plant–pathogen interaction, photosynthesis and starch and sucrose metabolism (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#Fig1">1d</a>). Conversely, significant decreases in gene families were observed from the two lineages of Viridiplantae to Rhodophyta (41 and 44, respectively), mainly corresponding to purine metabolism, endocytosis and pentose and glucuronate interconversions. In comparison, significant increases and decreases in gene families between streptophyte algae and Chlorophyta were more modest: the main increases for gene families in streptophyte algae were in plant hormone signal transduction, plant–pathogen interactions and TFs, whereas the main decreases for gene families were in purine and pyrimidine metabolism, DNA replication and O-glycan biosynthesis.</p><p>In addition, our analysis revealed a higher percentage of embryophyte genes in <i>M. viride</i> and <i>C. atmophyticus</i> compared to Chlorophyta, but lower than in <i>K. nitens</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#Fig1">1e</a>). In addition to gene families, the total number of conserved Pfam domains in Rhodophyta, Chlorophyta and Streptophyta was subjected to principal component analysis, which showed distinct patterns of functional diversification indicating evolutionary diversification (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#Fig1">1f</a>). Overall, these results suggest that the genomes of early-diverging streptophyte algae already contained archetypal genes that typically exist in modern embryophytes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">TFs and phytohormones in early-diverging Streptophyta</h3><p>Out of 114 types of TFs/transcription regulators (TRs) analysed, 72 (<i>M. viride)</i> and 80 (<i>C. atmophyticus)</i> TFs/TRs were identified in the two genomes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#Fig2">2a</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#MOESM3">18</a>), of which most are associated with abiotic stress responses, development and plant–pathogen interactions in embryophytes. TF/TR genes accounted for 3.31% of the total number of protein-coding genes in the <i>M. viride</i> genome and 4.47% in the <i>C. atmophyticus</i> genome, similar to the percentages in bryophytes (4.68%) and angiosperms (~5%). A combination of HMMER and phylogenetic analyses (for details see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#Sec10">Methods</a>) revealed putative gains of several TF/TR genes in the common ancestor of streptophytes, and differential losses of some of these genes in <i>M. viride</i> and/or <i>C. atmophyticus</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#Fig2">2b</a>). We cannot, however, exclude the possibility that the lower TF/TR numbers in <i>M. viride</i> relate to lack of representation in the genome assembly, although we aimed to compensate for this by adding data from our deeply sequenced transcriptome. Among the gains, the homeodomain–leucine zipper (HD-ZIP) family members are unique to plants and have diverse functions in growth and development, mostly related to stress responses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Ariel, F. D., Manavella, P. A., Dezar, C. A. &amp; Chan, R. L. The true story of the HD-Zip family. Trends Plant Sci. 12, 419–426 (2007)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d57113e1242">19</a></sup>. Of the four known classes (I–IV) of HD-ZIPs, gene classes I–III were found in <i>C. atmophyticus</i> but were missing in <i>M. viride</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#Fig2">2a</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#MOESM1">6</a>). <i>M</i>. <i>viride</i> displayed one gene that was positioned near the base of the HD-ZIPIV clade in phylogenetic analyses with moderate support and a long branch (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#MOESM1">6</a>). However, this gene contained neither a HD nor a START domain, suggesting that it represents either an ancestral pre-HD-ZIP_IV or a degenerated gene with domain, and perhaps functional, loss. Other TF/TR genes that apparently originated in the common ancestor of streptophytes, such as auxin response factors (ARF) proto-C-type (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#Fig2">2b</a> and Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#MOESM1">7</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#MOESM1">8</a>), SHI-related sequence (SRS), Trihelix TF family (Trihelix), growth-regulating factors (GRF), LUG transcriptional co-repressor (LEUNIG) and HD-PLINC homeodomain plant zinc finger (HD-PLINC), have also been implicated in the regulation of growth and development in response to light, salt and pathogen stresses in embryophytes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Bouzroud, S. et al. Auxin response factors (ARFs) are potential mediators of auxin action in tomato response to biotic and abiotic stress (Solanum lycopersicum). PLoS ONE 13, e0193517 (2018)." href="#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d57113e1277">20</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Guan, H. et al. Genome-wide identification, phylogeny analysis, expression profiling, and determination of protein–protein interactions of the LEUNIG gene family members in tomato. Gene 679, 1–10 (2018)." href="#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d57113e1277_1">21</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Kaplan-Levy, R. N., Brewer, P. B., Quon, T. &amp; Smyth, D. R. The trihelix family of transcription factors – light, stress and development. Trends Plant Sci. 17, 163–171 (2012)." href="#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d57113e1277_2">22</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Mukherjee, K., Brocchieri, L. &amp; Bürglin, T. R. A comprehensive classification and evolutionary analysis of plant homeobox genes. Mol. Biol. Evol. 26, 2775–2794 (2009)." href="#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d57113e1277_3">23</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Omidbakhshfard, M. A., Proost, S., Fujikura, U. &amp; Mueller-Roeber, B. Growth-regulating factors (GRFs): a small transcription factor family with important functions in plant biology. Mol. Plant 8, 998–1010 (2015)." href="#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d57113e1277_4">24</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Paul, A. et al. RNA-seq-mediated transcriptome analysis of actively growing and winter dormant shoots identifies non-deciduous habit of evergreen tree tea during winters. Sci. Rep. 4, 5932 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d57113e1280">25</a></sup>. In addition to gains, nine TF/TR gene families expanded by a minimum of threefold in <i>M. viride</i>/<i>C. atmophyticus</i> compared to non-streptophytes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#MOESM3">18</a>), and five of those (C2C2_CO-like, DDT, PcG_FIE, PcG_MSI, PSEUDO-ARR-B) had a minimum of 50% more members in <i>C. atmophyticus</i> than in <i>M. viride</i>, all involved in responses to abiotic and biotic stresses in embryophytes. Overall, our results correlate well with a recent analysis using transcriptome data<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Wilhelmsson, P. K. I., Mühlich, C., Ullrich, K. K. &amp; Rensing, S. A. Comprehensive genome-wide classification reveals that many plant-specific transcription factors evolved in streptophyte algae. Genome Biol. Evol. 9, 3384–3397 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d57113e1300">13</a></sup>, except that we extend the origin of HD-Zip genes I–III, ARF and HD-KNOX1 (Knotted-like homebox class I) to the common ancestor of streptophytes rather than a later origin.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Analysis of TF genes and phytohormone signalling pathways in M. viride and C. atmophyticus."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: Analysis of TF genes and phytohormone signalling pathways in <i>M. viride</i> and <i>C. atmophyticus</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-019-0560-3/MediaObjects/41477_2019_560_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-019-0560-3/MediaObjects/41477_2019_560_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="600" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Using a HMMER approach for the respective genomes, the numbers of TFs and TRs were identified using the TAPscan database v.2 (for details see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#Sec10">Methods</a>). <b>b</b>, Illustrative phylogenetic representation of the predicted gain (green) and loss (orange) of plant TFs in streptophyte algae. <b>c</b>, Presence/absence of the main phytohormone signalling pathways deduced from the genomes of <i>M. viride</i> and <i>C. atmophyticus</i>. Coloured boxes indicate the presence of genes in the pathways, white boxes their absence. All searches were done using HMM (1 × 10<sup>–10</sup>). Purple-lined ellipses denote genes identified in <i>M. viride</i> but not in <i>C. atmophyticus</i>, while the green-lined ellipse denotes a gene identified in <i>C. atmophyticus</i> but not in <i>M. viride</i>. <b>d</b>, The maximum-likelihood method was used to draw the phylogenetic tree of the PIN and PIN-related homologues to understand their origin among Streptophyta.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Next, we explored phytohormone biosynthesis and transduction pathway-related genes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#Fig2">2c</a> and Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#MOESM3">19</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#MOESM3">20</a>). By employing a HMMER search, an almost intact abscisic acid (ABA) signalling pathway was detected although the ABA receptor PYL was absent, the latter finding consistent with earlier reports on <i>K. nitens</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Hori, K. et al. Klebsormidium flaccidum genome reveals primary factors for plant terrestrial adaptation. Nat. Commun. 5, 3978 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d57113e1370">6</a></sup> and <i>C. braunii</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Nishiyama, T. et al. The Chara genome: secondary complexity and implications for plant terrestrialization. Cell 174, 448–464 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d57113e1377">7</a></sup>. Homologues of the Abscisic acid responsive element-binding protein (AREB)/Abscisic acid responsive element-binding factors (ABFs) TFs involved in ABA signalling under drought stress in embyophytes were also detected in both genomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Yoshida, T., Mogami, J. &amp; Yamaguchi-Shinozaki, K. ABA-dependent and ABA-independent signaling in response to osmotic stress in plants. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 21, 133–139 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d57113e1381">26</a></sup>. Genes for the complete signalling pathway of cytokinin (CK<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Brenner, W. G., Romanov, G. A., Köllmer, I., Bürkle, L. &amp; Schmülling, T. Immediate-early and delayed cytokinin response genes of Arabidopsis thaliana identified by genome-wide expression profiling reveal novel cytokinin-sensitive processes and suggest cytokinin action through transcriptional cascades. Plant J. 44, 314–333 (2005)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d57113e1385">27</a></sup>) were identified in both genomes. The second, conserved, two-component signalling pathway (in addition to CK), that of ethylene, was also almost completely represented except for the TF EIN3/EIL.</p><p>Both <i>M. viride</i> and <i>C. atmophyticus</i> were found to encode various transporter proteins and ATP-binding cassette B transporters that could potentially participate in auxin transport (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#MOESM3">21</a>). However, no auxin receptor (TIR) was found in either genome (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#Fig2">2c</a>). Notably, PIN, an important auxin efflux carrier involved in the transport of auxin between cells, was detected in the <i>M. viride</i> genome but not in <i>C. atmophyticus</i>. We found a step-by-step gain in motifs corresponding to PIN (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#Fig2">2d</a>). Neither <i>M. viride</i> nor <i>C. atmophyticus</i> encodes the auxin signal transduction components AUX/IAA1, SAUR and GH3 (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#Fig2">2c</a>). Furthermore, both genomes lacked auxin biosynthesis genes such as tryptophan-aminotransferase (TAA) and nitrilase, while YUCCA could be identified in <i>M. viride</i> only (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#MOESM3">19</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#MOESM1">9</a>).</p><p>Both organisms could synthesize most of the jasmonic acid (JA)-precursor OPDA but lacked OPR3, indicating that the JA biosynthetic pathway may exist in early-diverging streptophyte algae (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#MOESM3">19</a>). Concomitantly, both <i>C. atmophyticus</i> and <i>M. viride</i> lacked the complete JA and strigolactone (SL)) signalling components and the respective hormone receptors (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#Fig2">2c</a>). Gibberellic acid (GA) receptors (GID1 and GID2) and signalling components (DELLA, PIL1 and PIF) were also absent (for the PIF and bHLH phylogeny, see Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#MOESM1">10</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#MOESM1">11</a>). Some components of the salicylic acid (SA) pathway (CUL3 and TGA) were, however, detected (for TGA see Supplementary Figs <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#MOESM1">12</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#MOESM1">13</a>). Finally, we analysed sequence conservation of phytohormone receptor genes among representative algal lineages (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#Fig7">2</a>). The results showed that, although the genomes of <i>M. viride</i> and <i>C. atmophyticus</i> encode some phytohormone-related receptors, they are not as well conserved as in embryophytes. In general, our results are in accordance with previous genome analyses of later-diverging streptophyte algae and liverworts<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Bowman, J. L. et al. Insights into land plant evolution garnered from the Marchantia polymorpha genome. Cell 171, 287–304 (2017)." href="#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d57113e1471">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Hori, K. et al. Klebsormidium flaccidum genome reveals primary factors for plant terrestrial adaptation. Nat. Commun. 5, 3978 (2014)." href="#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d57113e1471_1">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Nishiyama, T. et al. The Chara genome: secondary complexity and implications for plant terrestrialization. Cell 174, 448–464 (2018)." href="#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d57113e1471_2">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Bowman, J. L., Briginshaw, L. N., Fisher, T. J. &amp; Flores-Sandoval, E. Something ancient and something neofunctionalized—evolution of land plant hormone signaling pathways. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 47, 64–72 (2019)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d57113e1474">8</a></sup> which suggested that F-box-mediated phytohormone signalling pathways (auxin, JA, GA, SL) evolved in the embryophyte ancestor, whereas two-component system phytohormone signalling (CK, ethylene) evolved much earlier, having been present in the common ancestor of streptophytes (this study). Furthermore, some components of both F-box-mediated (auxin, SA) and other phytohormone signalling pathways (ABA, brassinosteroid (BR)) are ancient and presumably originated in the common ancestor of streptophytes—for example, PIN, C-type ARF and TGA (this study).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Analysis of cell wall metabolism, evidence of sexual reproduction and analysis of flagellar genes</h3><p>In total, 81 and 100 putative carbohydrate-active enzymes (CAZymes) and 20 and 22 additional proteins containing putative carbohydrate-binding modules were identified in <i>M. viride</i> and <i>C. atmophyticus</i>, respectively (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#MOESM3">22</a>). Both the numbers of glycosyl hydrolases (GH) and glycosyltransferases (GT) were higher in <i>C. atmophyticus</i> than in <i>M. viride</i>, but in the same range as those found in unicellular red algae (32/54) or early-diverging Chlorophyta<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Ulvskov, P., Paiva, D. S., Domozych, D. &amp; Harholt, J. Classification, naming and evolutionary history of glycosyltransferases from sequenced green and red algal genomes. PLoS ONE 8, e76511 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d57113e1502">28</a></sup> (52/57/77). Compared to previous transcriptome-based analyses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Mikkelsen, M. D. et al. Evidence for land plant cell wall biosynthetic mechanisms in charophyte green algae. Ann. Bot. 114, 1217–1236 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d57113e1506">12</a></sup>, the differences in numbers of GTs between <i>M. viride</i> and <i>C. atmophyticus</i> were not as large, suggesting that hidden life history stages in <i>M. viride</i> (zygotes?) may contribute GTs to its overall genomic GT complement. The largest GT families found were GT2 (<i>N</i>-glycosylation and cell wall biosynthesis), GT5 (starch synthases) and GT35 (glycogen/starch phosphorylases). However, certain differences were also encountered: GT 77 (involved in rhamnogalacturonan-II synthesis) had more family members in <i>C. atmophyticus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Petersen, B. L., Faber, K. &amp; Ulvskov, P. Glycosyltransferases of the GT77 family. Ann. Plant Rev. 41, 305–320 (2010)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d57113e1525">29</a></sup>, while GT 41 (β-<i>N</i>-acetylglucosaminyltransferase) was more prominent in <i>M. viride</i>. Four GT families were present in only <i>M. viride</i> or <i>C. atmophyticus</i>, among them GT8 (in <i>C. atmophyticus</i>), a large family of glycosyltransferases in embryophytes, of which some members have been implicated in responses to abiotic stress<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="Cheng, L. et al. Expressional characterization of galacturonosyltransferase-like gene family in Eucalyptus grandis implies a role in abiotic stress responses. Tree Genet. Genomes 14, 81 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d57113e1545">30</a></sup>.</p><p>To gain further insight into the evolution of proteins involved in cell wall biosynthesis, we analysed the phylogeny of representative enzymes. Our analyses revealed the presence of three putative cellulose synthase-like (CSL) enzymes (CSLA/CSLC-like), but the absence of cellulose synthase (CESA), in <i>M. viride</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#Fig3">3a</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#MOESM3">23</a>). However, three CESA/CSLD-like homologues were identified in the <i>C. atmophyticus</i> genome and showed high similarity to the respective genes in embryophytes. <i>C. atmophyticus</i> displayed two additional CSL enzymes that apparently originated by a gene duplication in the common ancestor of <i>M. viride</i> and <i>C. atmophyticus</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#Fig3">3a</a>). Our phylogenetic analysis demonstrated that CESA and CSL proteins diverged into two clades, their origin apparently dating back to the ancestor of the Archaeplastida<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Brawley, S. H. et al. Insights into the red algae and eukaryotic evolution from the genome of Porphyra umbilicalis (Bangiophyceae, Rhodophyta). Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 114, E6361–E6370 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d57113e1577">31</a></sup>. The first clade encompassed CESA and CLSB, -D, -E, -G and -H, the second clade CSLA, CSLC and a paraphyletic set of genes (CSLA/CSLC-like) that includes five CSL genes from <i>M. viride</i> and <i>C. atmophyticus</i>. Mapping CESA and CSLs on a simplified phylogenetic tree of Archaeplastida pinpoints putative gains and losses of these enzymes on different branches (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#Fig3">3b</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Phylogenetic distribution of enzymes involved in cell wall biosynthesis in M. viride and C. atmophyticus."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: Phylogenetic distribution of enzymes involved in cell wall biosynthesis in <i>M. viride</i> and <i>C. atmophyticus</i>.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-019-0560-3/MediaObjects/41477_2019_560_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-019-0560-3/MediaObjects/41477_2019_560_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="799" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Phylogenetic tree of CESA and CSL (GT2 family). The tree was constructed using the maximum-likelihood method. <b>b</b>, Left: summary of the gains and losses of cell wall biosynthetic genes mapped on the phylogenetic tree. Right: copy number of the respective cell wall biosynthetic genes. HG, homogalacturonan; XGA, xylogalacturonan; RGI and RGII, rhamnogalacturonan I and II; AGP, arabinogalactan protein; GSL, glucan synthase-like.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>To adapt to rapidly changing environments, early-diverging streptophytes would be expected to actively conduct reassembly and degradation of cell wall components to enhance the flexibility of the cell wall during osmotic stress. Enzymes in 14 families of glycoside hydrolases (GH5-10, -12, -26, -44, -45, -48, -51, -61 and -74) are known to degrade cellulose in embryophytes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Minic, Z. &amp; Jouanin, L. Plant glycoside hydrolases involved in cell wall polysaccharide degradation. Plant Physiol. Biochem. 44, 435–449 (2006)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d57113e1615">32</a></sup>. Not surprisingly, cellulases were not detected in the <i>M. viride</i> genome, which lacks CESA; however, in <i>C. atmophyticus</i> cellulases were present (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#Tab1">1</a>). Interestingly, a majority of the genes involved in mannan and xylan metabolism, such as mannanases, mannosidase and xylosidase, were detected in <i>M. viride</i> whereas <i>C. atmophyticus</i> lacks those enzymes. <i>M. viride</i> and <i>C. atmophyticu</i>s also lack xyloglucan- and xylan-degrading enzymes, as well as most of the pectin lyases (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#Tab1">1</a>).</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 GH families involved in degradation of cell wall components present in <i>M. viride</i>, <i>C. atmophyticus</i> and <i>K. nitens</i></b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Some of the differences in the composition of the cell surface between <i>M. viride</i> and <i>C. atmophyticus</i> could be related to variation in their cellular organization, life history and habitats. To check whether ‘cryptic sex’ may exist in early-diverging streptophyte algae, we searched the <i>M. viride</i> and <i>C. atmophyticus</i> genomes for meiosis-specific (11) and meiosis-related genes (40) by hidden Markov model (HMM) and BLAST (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#MOESM3">24</a>). We found that the core-set of meiosis-specific genes (10) was present in <i>C. atmophyticus</i> whereas the <i>M. viride</i> genome lacked MSH5, REC8 and RED1 (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#MOESM3">24</a>); the absence of the latter is puzzling, but not without precedent<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Tekle, Y. I., Wood, F. C., Katz, L. A., Cerón-Romero, M. A. &amp; Gorfu, L. A. Amoebozoans are secretly but ancestrally sexual: evidence for sex genes and potential novel crossover pathways in diverse groups of amoebae. Genome Biol. Evol. 9, 375–387 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d57113e1900">33</a></sup>. <i>M. viride</i> reproduces vegetatively by binary division of the flagellate cell, whereas <i>C. atmophyticus</i> forms scale-covered zoospores during asexual reproduction. A comparative analysis of the complement of flagellar genes in their genomes, using a stringent reciprocal-best-BLAST-hits analysis of 397 <i>Chlamydomonas</i> flagellar proteins as query<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Nevers, Y. et al. Insights into ciliary genes and evolution from multi-level phylogenetic profiling. Mol. Biol. Evol. 34, 2016–2034 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d57113e1914">34</a></sup>, detected 204 flagellar proteins in <i>M. viride</i> and 192 in <i>C. atmophyticus</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#Fig4">4</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#MOESM3">25</a>). Non-flagellate organisms lack the majority of radial spoke, as well as central pair proteins, many of the outer and inner dynein arm proteins and all intraflagellar transport proteins (IFTs), as well as dynein heavy-chain proteins. Surprisingly, in both <i>M. viride</i> and <i>C. atmophyticus</i> (flagella covered by scales), we could identify only a few IFTs whereas <i>K. nitens</i> displayed the full set (12 IFTs) (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#Fig8">3</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Analysis of flagellar genes and their phylogenetic distribution."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: Analysis of flagellar genes and their phylogenetic distribution.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3/figures/4" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-019-0560-3/MediaObjects/41477_2019_560_Fig4_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-4-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-019-0560-3/MediaObjects/41477_2019_560_Fig4_HTML.png" alt="figure4" loading="lazy" width="685" height="656" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-4-desc"><p>The phylogenomic tree (left) was constructed using a maximum-likelihood method based on the concatenated sequences of single-copy genes from different representative algal lineages. The horizontal bar chart (middle) denotes the number of putative orthologues to 398 <i>Chlamydomonas</i> conserved flagellar proteins; the pink horizontal bar represents the number of structure-related flagellar genes (individual genes listed at the top of the right panel), while the green area represents the number of flagella-associated genes. The right panel shows the key structure-related flagellar proteins in six categories. The circle size is proportional to the copy number of putative orthologous genes found in the respective species.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Evolutionary analysis of elongation factor-1α and phytochromes</h3><p>The elongation factor EF-1α is responsible for the selection and binding of aminoacyl-transfer RNA to the A-site (acceptor site) of the ribosome. It is substituted by elongation factor-like (EF-like) proteins in many eukaryotes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Keeling, P. J. &amp; Inagaki, Y. A class of eukaryotic GTPase with a punctate distribution suggesting multiple functional replacements of translation elongation factor 1. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 101, 15380–15385 (2004)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d57113e1964">35</a></sup>. Intriguingly, we found that the <i>M. viride</i> genome encodes both EF-1α and EF-like genes (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#Fig5">5a</a> and Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#Fig9">4</a>), contradicting earlier studies that reported only the presence of EF-like genes in <i>M. viride</i> and no EF-1α (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 36" title="Cocquyt, E. et al. Gain and loss of elongation factor genes in green algae. BMC Evol. Biol. 9, 39 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d57113e1981">36</a></sup>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Distribution of EF-1α, EF-like and plant phytochromes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5: Distribution of EF-1α, EF-like and plant phytochromes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-019-0560-3/MediaObjects/41477_2019_560_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-019-0560-3/MediaObjects/41477_2019_560_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="554" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Maximum likelihood was used to infer the phylogenetic tree. Right: representative EF-1α and EF-like motifs, from red algae to embryophytes. ‘Other Streptophyta’ represents <i>C. atmophyticus</i>, Klebsormidiophyceae, Coleochaetophyceae, Charophyceae and Zygnematophyceae. <b>b</b>, Left: simplified phylogenic tree of phytochromes across cyanobacteria, glaucophytes, prasinophytes and streptophyte algae is shown. Right: complete domain structures of the phytochrome proteins. PHYX1/PHYX2 represent the sister lineage to p-PHY. The phytochromes from glaucophytes and cyanobacteria are represented by g-PHY and c-PHY, respectively.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>The origin of the canonical embryophyte phytochrome (p-PHY) can be traced to the ancestor of extant streptophyte algae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Li, F. W. et al. Phytochrome diversity in green plants and the origin of canonical plant phytochromes. Nat. Commun. 6, 7852 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d57113e2005">37</a></sup>. Two phytochrome genes were identified in both the <i>M. viride</i> and <i>C. atmophyticus</i> genome, which is in accordance with a previous transcriptome study<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Li, F. W. et al. Phytochrome diversity in green plants and the origin of canonical plant phytochromes. Nat. Commun. 6, 7852 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d57113e2015">37</a></sup> (Extended Data Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#Fig10">5</a>). Interestingly, the two genes of <i>M. viride</i> showed differing domain structures, one gene containing a response regulator at the C terminus regulatory module (REC), which was not identified in the previous transcriptome study<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Li, F. W. et al. Phytochrome diversity in green plants and the origin of canonical plant phytochromes. Nat. Commun. 6, 7852 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d57113e2026">37</a></sup>. Phylogenetic analyses indicated that a gene duplication occurred in the ancestor of streptophytes, followed by loss of REC in one of the duplicated genes, resulting in the evolution of p-PHY (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#Fig5">5b</a>). Non-canonical phytochromes (PHYX1 and PHYX2) were retained in several streptophyte algal lineages.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec8" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec8-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec8">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec8-content"><p>The successful colonization of the terrestrial landscape by plants, and their subsequent rapid evolution, is considered to be a pivotal event in the evolution of life. Here, we present the draft genomes of two early-diverging streptophyte algae that thrive in contrasting habitats: <i>M. viride</i> is found in the benthos of small shallow ponds, whereas <i>C. atmophyticus</i> is a subaerial/terrestrial alga. All previous phylogenomic analyses, including the present study, placed the two species as sister taxa in the earliest-diverging clade of streptophytes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Lemieux, C., Otis, C. &amp; Turmel, M. A clade uniting the green algae Mesostigma viride and Chlorokybus atmophyticus represents the deepest branch of the streptophyta in chloroplast genome-based phylogenies. BMC Biol. 5, 2 (2007)." href="#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d57113e2048">14</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Timme, R. E., Bachvaroff, T. R. &amp; Delwiche, C. F. Broad phylogenomic sampling and the sister lineage of land plants. PLoS ONE 7, e29696 (2012)." href="#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d57113e2048_1">15</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Wickett, N. J. et al. Phylotranscriptomic analysis of the origin and early diversification of land plants. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 111, E4859–E4868 (2014)." href="#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d57113e2048_2">16</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Wodniok, S. et al. Origin of land plants: do conjugating green algae hold the key? BMC Evol. Biol. 11, 104 (2011)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d57113e2051">17</a></sup>. Their contrasting habitats and phylogenetic position make the genomes of <i>M. viride</i> and <i>C. atmophyticus</i> an exciting resource for comparative investigations into land plant evolution. Our genome analyses suggest that early-diverging streptophyte algae already took the first step on the long road to plant terrestrialization and harbour many embryophyte-type genes. Compared to Chlorophyta, the main gene family gains and expansions in early-diverging streptophyte algae were in plant hormone signal transduction, plant–pathogen interactions and TFs and regulators, most related to environmental stresses (light, temperature, salt, drought and pathogens) that are thought to have played a major role in plant terrestrialization.</p><p><i>Mesostigma viride</i> and <i>C. atmophyticus</i> differ distinctively in their life history and cellular organization. To test whether these differences between the two species are responsible for their genome differences, we analysed the complement of flagellar genes. We detected 204 of 397 <i>Chlamydomonas</i> flagellar proteins in the <i>M. viride</i> genome. Similar numbers of conserved flagellar proteins exist in streptophyte algae that produce reproductive flagellate cells (zoospores, spermatozoids) during their life history (240 in <i>K. nitens</i> and 209 in <i>C. braunii</i>), suggesting that the ancestral number of flagellar proteins in Streptophyta was ~200–250. Second, we looked for possible differences in their life histories focusing on sexual reproduction (in <i>C. atmophyticus</i> sexual reproduction is unknown, in <i>M. viride</i> only a preliminary report exists). The core-set of meiosis-specific genes (10) were present in <i>C. atmophyticus</i>, whereas the <i>M. viride</i> genome lacked three of them. This suggested that both species reproduce sexually, although the process itself remains poorly understood.</p><p>Further insight into the differences in gene composition between <i>M. viride</i> and <i>C. atmophyticus</i> was obtained by focusing on genes involved in adaptations to the different habitats in which both species thrive. With the streptophyte algal phylogeny basically resolved, we mapped TF/TR genes on the phylogenetic tree and determined putative gains, losses, expansions and contractions of TF/TR gene family members. From this, we conclude that TFs/TRs that regulate growth and development in responses to light, salt and pathogen stresses in embryophytes originated in the common ancestor of streptophytes. Interestingly, some of the TFs/TRs thought to be involved in responses to light, salt and pathogen stresses, such as the HD-ZIPs_I–II, SRS and GRF, were absent in <i>M. viride</i>, perhaps lost in relation to its aquatic habitat. This hypothesis is consistent with the previous genome analysis of <i>C. braunii</i> (a structurally complex, streptophyte alga from an aquatic, benthic habitat) that also seems to have lost an HD-ZIP gene (HD-ZIP_IV), another HD-gene (HD_KNOX 1) and GRF<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Nishiyama, T. et al. The Chara genome: secondary complexity and implications for plant terrestrialization. Cell 174, 448–464 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d57113e2109">7</a></sup>; HD-ZIP genes have also been lost in some secondarily aquatic embryophytes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Romani, F., Reinheimer, R., Florent, S. N., Bowman, J. L. &amp; Moreno, J. E. Evolutionary history of HOMEODOMAIN LEUCINE ZIPPER transcription factors during plant transition to land. New Phytol. 219, 408–421 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d57113e2114">38</a></sup>.</p><p>Although elements of several biosynthetic and signalling phytohormone pathways were identified in <i>M. viride</i> and <i>C. atmophyticus</i>, they were mostly incomplete and only the CK pathway, considered to be involved in abiotic stress responses<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Bari, R. &amp; Jones, J. D. G. Role of plant hormones in plant defence responses. Plant Mol. Biol. 69, 473–488 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d57113e2127">39</a></sup>, was fully recovered. Ethylene-related signalling homologues have previously been reported in <i>Spirogyra pratensis</i> and <i>Coleochaete orbicularis</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Ju, C. et al. Conservation of ethylene as a plant hormone over 450 million years of evolution. Nat. Plants 1, 14004 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d57113e2136">40</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Van de Poel, B., Cooper, E. D., Van Der Straeten, D., Chang, C. &amp; Delwiche, C. F. Transcriptome profiling of the green alga Spirogyra pratensis (Charophyta) suggests an ancestral role for ethylene in cell wall metabolism, photosynthesis, and abiotic stress responses. Plant Physiol. 172, 533–545 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d57113e2139">41</a></sup>, and this is consistent with our findings. <i>K. nitens</i> also displays most of the ethylene-related genes, including the TF EIN3/EIL which is lacking in <i>M. viride</i> and <i>C. atmophyticus</i>, suggesting that this TF had its origin in the common ancestor of <i>K. nitens</i> and derived streptophytes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Hori, K. et al. Klebsormidium flaccidum genome reveals primary factors for plant terrestrial adaptation. Nat. Commun. 5, 3978 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d57113e2156">6</a></sup>. The ABA pathway was also nearly complete but lacked the receptor PYL. A PYL orthologue was recently identified in a transcriptomic analysis of the streptophyte alga <i>Zygnema</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="de Vries, J., Curtis, B. A., Gould, S. B. &amp; Archibald, J. M. Embryophyte stress signaling evolved in the algal progenitors of land plants. Proc. Natl Acad. Sci. USA 115, E3471–E3480 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d57113e2163">9</a></sup> (Zygnematophyceae). The absence of the F-box-mediated AUXIN, JA, GA and SL signalling pathways in the early-diverging streptophyte algae is not surprising, as previous studies have reported orthologues of most of the signalling components of these pathways in genomes of embryophytes but not of algae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Bowman, J. L. et al. Insights into land plant evolution garnered from the Marchantia polymorpha genome.
