Cell 171, 287–304 (2017)." href="#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d57113e2167">5</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Hori, K. et al. Klebsormidium flaccidum genome reveals primary factors for plant terrestrial adaptation. Nat. Commun. 5, 3978 (2014)." href="#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d57113e2167_1">6</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Nishiyama, T. et al. The Chara genome: secondary complexity and implications for plant terrestrialization. Cell 174, 448–464 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d57113e2170">7</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 42" title="Han, G. Z. Evolution of jasmonate biosynthesis and signalling mechanisms. J. Exp. Bot. 68, 1323–1331 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d57113e2173">42</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 43" title="Ohtaka, K., Hori, K., Kanno, Y., Seo, M. &amp; Ohta, H. Primitive auxin response without TIR1 and Aux/IAA in the charophyte alga Klebsormidium nitens. Plant Physiol. 174, 1621–1632 (2017)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d57113e2176">43</a></sup>. Interestingly, we identified the origin of several signalling components involved in phytohormone pathways. The PIN protein, an important auxin efflux carrier<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Křeček, P. et al. The PIN-FORMED (PIN) protein family of auxin transporters. Genome Biol. 10, 249 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d57113e2180">44</a></sup>, seems to have originated in the common ancestor of streptophytes (two PINs were detected in <i>M. viride</i>) or even in the common ancestor of Viridiplantae (contrary to a previous study, we detected the presence of PIN also in the Chlorellaceae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Viaene, T., Delwiche, C. F., Rensing, S. A. &amp; Friml, J. Origin and evolution of PIN auxin transporters in the green lineage. Trends Plant Sci. 18, 5–10 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d57113e2187">45</a></sup> (Chlorophyta)).</p><p>Previous studies have shown that the most important core cell wall polysaccharides of embryophytes, namely cellulose, mannan, xyloglucan, xylan and pectin, are also represented in diverse lineages of streptophyte algae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Mikkelsen, M. D. et al. Evidence for land plant cell wall biosynthetic mechanisms in charophyte green algae. Ann. Bot. 114, 1217–1236 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d57113e2195">12</a></sup>. The cellulose synthase-like gene families are among the most important players involved in the formation of plant cell walls<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Yin, Y., Huang, J. &amp; Xu, Y. The cellulose synthase superfamily in fully sequenced plants and algae. BMC Plant Biol. 9, 99 (2009)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d57113e2199">46</a></sup>. Three CESA/CSLD-like homologues were identified in the <i>C. atmophyticus</i> genome, which showed high similarity to the respective genes in embryophytes. Our analyses also revealed the presence of putative CSL enzymes (CSLA/CSLC-like) in both genomes, but no CESA in <i>M. viride</i>, the latter corroborating a previous analysis using expressed sequence tags<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Simon, A., Glöckner, G., Felder, M., Melkonian, M. &amp; Becker, B. EST analysis of the scaly green flagellate Mesostigma viride (Streptophyta): implications for the evolution of green plants (Viridiplantae). BMC Plant Biol. 6, 1–13 (2006)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d57113e2209">47</a></sup>. Our phylogenetic analysis indicated that the CESAs and CSLDs of embryophytes were derived from CESA/CSLD-like homologues present in the common ancestor of streptophytes that were lost in <i>M. viride</i>.</p><p>Moreover, homologues of biosynthetic genes for Rhamnogalacturonan-I and -II (RG), which are considered among the evolutionarily youngest cell wall polysaccharides of embryophytes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Mikkelsen, M. D. et al. Evidence for land plant cell wall biosynthetic mechanisms in charophyte green algae. Ann. Bot. 114, 1217–1236 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d57113e2220">12</a></sup>, such as RGXT and GALSs, were also identified in <i>C. atmophyticus</i> but not in <i>M. viride</i>. We also found cellulases in both <i>C. atmophyticus</i> and <i>K</i>. <i>nitens</i> but not in <i>M. viride</i>, indicating that <i>M. viride</i> lost these enzymes together with cellulose when adapting to a flagellate life history and an aquatic habitat. It is likely that mannan performs the function of cellulose in <i>M. viride</i> and may be restricted to a putative zygotic stage. Xyloglucan and xylan presumably evolved later (in either the common ancestor of <i>K. nitens</i> and other streptophytes or in the ancestor of derived streptophyte algae<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Mikkelsen, M. D. et al. Evidence for land plant cell wall biosynthetic mechanisms in charophyte green algae. Ann. Bot. 114, 1217–1236 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d57113e2252">12</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Del-Bem, L. E. Xyloglucan evolution and the terrestrialization of green plants. New Phytol. 219, 1150–1153 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d57113e2255">48</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Jensen, J. K. et al. Identification of an algal xylan synthase indicates that there is functional orthology between algal and plant cell wall biosynthesis. New Phytol. 218, 1049–1060 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d57113e2258">49</a></sup>). In summary, <i>M. viride</i> and <i>C. atmophyticus</i> differ considerably in their CAZymes, as expected from variation in their cellular organization, life history and habitats, but also from later-diverging streptophyte algae such as <i>K. nitens</i>.</p><p>Our analyses of phytochromes in the genomes of <i>M. viride</i> and <i>C. atmophyticus</i> corroborate previous transcriptome studies<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 37" title="Li, F. W. et al. Phytochrome diversity in green plants and the origin of canonical plant phytochromes. Nat. Commun. 6, 7852 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d57113e2281">37</a></sup>, in that the origin of the canonical embryophyte phytochrome (p-PHY) can be traced to the common ancestor of streptophytes. We tentatively identified a gene duplication event in the common ancestor of streptophytes, followed by loss of the response regulator (REC) and the histidine phosphorylation site (H) in one of the duplicated genes. Notably, both phytochrome genes of <i>C. atmophyticus</i> clustered with canonical embryophyte phytochromes (p-PHY) as sisters of p-PHY of <i>M. viride</i>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec9" data-title="Conclusions"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec9-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec9">Conclusions</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec9-content"><p>Two major conclusions can be drawn from the comparative analysis of the draft genomes of <i>M. viride</i> and <i>C. atmophyticus</i>: first, the common ancestor of <i>M. viride</i> and <i>C. atmophyticus</i> had already developed traits that reflect adaptations to a subaerial/terrestrial habitat, exemplified by the presence of the canonical embryophyte photoreceptor phytochrome (p-PHY), evolution of TFs implicated in responses to various abiotic and biotic stresses, near-complete pathways for several phytohormones involved in stress signalling and evolution of orthologues of cellulose synthase and cellulose synthase-like genes characteristic of embryophytes. The common ancestor of streptophytes had thus taken the first step toward plant terrestrialization, supporting a recent hypothesis that streptophyte algae lived on land before the emergence of embryophytes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Harholt, J., Moestrup, Ø. &amp; Ulvskov, P. Why plants were terrestrial from the beginning. Trends Plant Sci. 21, 96–101 (2016)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d57113e2311">50</a></sup>.</p><p>Second, the genomes of <i>M. viride</i> and <i>C. atmophyticus</i> differ conspicuously in genome size, structure and gene complement, revealing the dynamic nature of their genomes perhaps in response to adaptations to their contrasting habitats. Furthermore, the phylogenetic relationship of <i>M. viride</i> and <i>C. atmophyticus</i> as sister taxa leads to the conclusion that <i>M. viride</i> lost several traits of a subaerial/terrestrial ancestry following transition to a benthic, freshwater habitat, a situation that finds a parallel in the secondarily aquatic <i>C. braunii</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Nishiyama, T. et al. The Chara genome: secondary complexity and implications for plant terrestrialization. Cell 174, 448–464 (2018)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d57113e2336">7</a></sup>. Although adaptations to terrestrial life progressed stepwise in streptophyte algae, this did not follow a linear path with deviations into aquatic habitats apparently occurring repeatedly, requiring careful comparative genomic and phylogenomic analyses of a larger taxon set of streptophyte algae in future studies.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec10" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec10-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec10">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec10-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">Culture, nucleic acid extraction and light microscopy</h3><p>Axenic cultures of <i>M. viride</i> (CCAC 1140) and <i>C. atmophyticus</i> (CCAC 0220) were obtained from the Culture Collection of Algae at the University of Cologne, and grown in Waris-H culture medium<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="McFadden, G. I. &amp; Melkonian, M. Use of Hepes buffer for microalgal culture media and fixation for electron microscopy. Phycologia 25, 551–557 (1986)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d57113e2358">51</a></sup> (<a href="http://www.ccac.uni-koeln.de/">http://www.ccac.uni-koeln.de/</a>). During all steps of culture scale-up until nucleic acid extraction, axenicity was monitored by both sterility tests and light microscopy. Total RNA was extracted from <i>M. viride</i> using the Tri Reagent Method, and from <i>C. atmophyticus</i> using the CTAB-PVP method as described in ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Johnson, M. T. J. et al. Evaluating methods for isolating total RNA and predicting the success of sequencing phylogenetically diverse plant transcriptomes. PLoS ONE 7, e50226 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d57113e2373">52</a></sup>. Total DNA was extracted using a modified CTAB protocol<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Rogers, S. O. &amp; Bendich, A. J. Extraction of DNA from milligram amounts of fresh, herbarium and mummified plant tissues. Plant Mol. Biol. 5, 69–76 (1985)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d57113e2377">53</a></sup> (details below). Light microscopy was performed with a Leica DMLB light microscope using a PL-APO ×100/1.40 numerical aperture (NA) objective, an immersed condenser (NA, 1.4) and a Metz Mecablitz 32 Ct3 flash system.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Genome sequencing, data preparation and genome assembly</h3><p>Paired-end libraries with insert sizes of 170 bp, 200 bp, 500 bp, 800 bp, 2 kb, 5 kb, 10 kb and 20 kb were constructed following standard Illumina protocols. The libraries were sequenced on an Illumina HiSeq 2000/4000 and BGI-seq 500 platform. A total of 245 Gb (about 746.86X) and 66.46 Gb (about 775.68X) paired-end data were generated for <i>M. viride</i> (CCAC 1140) and <i>C. atmophyticus</i> (CCAC 0220), respectively. To reduce the effect of sequencing error on assembly, we performed a rigorous quality control of raw data. We used CLC Assembly Cell (v.5.0.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="White Paper on De Novo Assembly in CLC Assembly Cell 4.0 (CLC bio A/S, 2012)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d57113e2395">54</a></sup> to trim the adaptors, remove duplicates and trim low-quality bases. We then used Pairfq (v.0.16.0) (<a href="https://github.com/sestaton/Pairfq">https://github.com/sestaton/Pairfq</a>) to pair the reads. Finally, SOAPfilter (v.2.2) was used to filter the reads again. After filtering off duplicated and low-quality reads and those with adaptor sequences, 74.63 and 14.51 Gb high-quality clean reads remained for <i>M. viride</i> (CCAC1140) and <i>C. atmophyticus</i> (CCAC 0220), respectively, which were then subjected to a pipeline for genome assembly.</p><p><i>k</i>-mer analysis was performed to survey genome size, heterozygosity and repeat content before genome assembly. The peak of <i>k</i>-mer frequency (<i>F</i>) is determined by the total reads number (<i>N</i>), genome size (<i>G</i>), read length (<i>L</i>) and the length of <i>k</i>-mer (<i>K</i>), following the formula: <i>F</i> = <i>N</i> × (<i>L</i> – <i>K</i> + 1)/<i>G</i>. Total <i>k</i>-mer number (<i>M</i>) is determined by the formula <i>M</i> = <i>N</i> × (<i>L</i> – <i>K</i> + 1). As a result, genome size can be calculated by <i>G</i> = <i>M</i>/<i>F</i>. This formula enables accurate estimation of <i>G</i>, and hence an estimation of genome size for homozygous diploid or haploid genomes. All the above analyses indicated homozygosity of the genome and gave similar estimations of genome size. The final genome size estimate (329 Mb for <i>M. viride</i> and 85.68 Mb for <i>C. atmophyticus</i>) was obtained through 17-mer analysis.</p><p>We carried out the SPAdes (v.3.10.1)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Bankevich, A. et al. SPAdes: a new genome assembly algorithm and its applications to single-cell sequencing. J. Comput. Biol. 19, 455–477 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d57113e2494">55</a></sup> genome assembly algorithm to assemble the contigs of <i>M. viride</i>. The contigs were formed without gaps. Subsequently platanus (v.1.2.4) was conducted to construct the scaffolds, from short insert-sized paired-end reads to long insert-sized paired-end reads. To extend the assembly and close gaps, the scaffolder SSPACE (v.3.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Boetzer, M., Henkel, C. V., Jansen, H. J., Butler, D. &amp; Pirovano, W. Scaffolding pre-assembled contigs using SSPACE. Bioinformatics 27, 578–579 (2010)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d57113e2501">56</a></sup> was applied to extend the scaffolds while GapCloser (v.1.12) was used to close gaps and extend the scaffolds again. For <i>C. atmophyticus</i>, the assembly was generated by SOAPdenovo-127-mer (v.2.04)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Luo, R. et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. Gigascience 1, 18 (2012)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d57113e2508">57</a></sup> with a <i>k</i>-mer of 85. To close the gaps within the constructed scaffolds, we used paired-end reads mapped to the scaffolds using GapCloser (v.1.12). Variant detection was done using Pilon (v.2.11) to improve draft assembly quality.</p><p>The quality of the assembly was evaluated in four ways. First, we used BUSCO (v.3)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Simão, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210–3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d57113e2519">58</a></sup> to determine the proportion of a core-set of 303 highly conserved eukaryotic genes present in the genomes of <i>M. viride</i> and <i>C. atmophyticus</i>. Second, Soap (v.2.21) was used to map the reads to the draft assemblies to evaluate the DNA reads mapping rate in both species. Meanwhile, sequence depth and genetic copy content distribution were calculated. Third, we used BLAT (v.36)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Kent, W. J. BLAT—the BLAST-like alignment tool. Genome Res. 12, 656–664 (2002)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d57113e2529">59</a></sup> to compare the draft assemblies to a transcript assembled by Bridger. Finally, we mapped the RNA reads to the draft assemblies to evaluate the RNA reads mapping rate using Tophat2 (ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Kim, D. et al. TopHat2: accurate alignment of transcriptomes in the presence of insertions, deletions and gene fusions. Genome Biol. 14, R36 (2013)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d57113e2533">60</a></sup>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec13">Transcriptome sequencing and analysis</h3><p>For Illumina sequencing, we considered two ways of library construction. The ribosomal RNA-depleted RNA library was constructed using the ribo-zero rRNA removal kit (plant) (Illumina) following the manufacturer’s protocol, while the poly(A)-selected RNA library was constructed using the ScriptSeq Library Prep kit (Plant leaf) (Illumina) following the manufacturer’s protocol. These libraries were sequenced on the Illumina sequencing system. All of the sequenced data were then assembled into transcripts following the Bridger pipeline. This set of transcript sequences was used for assessing the accuracy of the genome assembly and for gene annotation.</p><p>Gene expression was measured as fragments per kilobase of transcript per million mapped reads.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">Detection and classification of repetitive elements</h3><p>Three types of repeat (DNA transposon elements, retrotransposon elements and tandem repeats) were identified in the genomes of <i>M. viride</i> and <i>C. atmophyticus</i>. DNA transposons and retrotransposon elements were identified using MITE-hunter<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Han, Y. &amp; Wessler, S. R. MITE-Hunter: a program for discovering miniature inverted-repeat transposable elements from genomic sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 38, e199–e199 (2010)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d57113e2562">61</a></sup> and LTRharvest<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Ellinghaus, D., Kurtz, S. &amp; Willhoeft, U. LTRharvest, an efficient and flexible software for de novo detection of LTR retrotransposons. BMC Bioinformatics 9, 18 (2008)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d57113e2566">62</a></sup>, respectively. RepeatModeler (v.1.0.8) was used to search for other repeats using a de novo approach. Alternatively, RepeatMasker<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Chen, N. Using repeatmasker to identify repetitive elements in genomic sequences. Curr. Protoc. Bioinformatics 5, 4–10 (2004)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d57113e2570">63</a></sup> was applied to using a custom library comprising a combination of Repbase and a de novo-predicted repetitive element library.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec15">Gene prediction</h3><p>We used a combination of de novo gene prediction methods, homology-based search methods and RNA sequencing-aided annotation methods. For de novo gene prediction, PASApipeline v.2.1.0 was applied to predict gene structure using transcripts assembled by Bridger, after which the inferred gene structures were used in AUGUSTUS (v.3.2.3)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="Stanke, M. et al. AUGUSTUS: ab initio prediction of alternative transcripts. Nucleic Acids Res. 34, W435–W439 (2006)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d57113e2583">64</a></sup> to train gene models based on transcript evidence. In addition, GeneMark (v.1.0)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Besemer, J. &amp; Borodovsky, M. GeneMark: web software for gene finding in prokaryotes, eukaryotes and viruses. Nucleic Acids Res. 33, W451–W454 (2005)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d57113e2587">65</a></sup> was used to build a hidden Markov model based on genome sequence. For homology-based annotation, we selected gene sets from certain model green algae. In regard to RNA sequencing-aided methods, we took the transcripts assembled by Bridger as evidence. The final consensus gene sets were generated by combining all the evidence using MAKER (v.2.31.8)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Campbell, M. S. et al. MAKER-P: a tool kit for the rapid creation, management, and quality control of plant genome annotations. Plant Physiol. 164, 513–524 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d57113e2591">66</a></sup>. The result of the first round was used for SNAP to train another hidden Markov model based on transcriptome. Subsequently, the hidden Markov model was added to MAKER.</p><p>The final gene set was evaluated with two approaches. The BUSCO core eukaryotic gene-mapping approach was used to determine gene set completeness; RNA read mapping was another means of evaluation. We mapped the RNA reads to the gene set with Tophat2, while coverage depth was calculated by Samtools (v.0.1.19).</p><p>Gene function annotation was performed by BLASTP (1 × 10<sup>–5</sup>) against several known databases, including SwissProt, TrEMBL, Kyoto Encyclopedia of Genes and Genomes (KEGG), COG and NR. InterProScan (using data from Pfam, PRINTS, SMART, ProDom and PROSITE) was used to identify protein motifs and protein domains of the predicted gene set. Gene Ontology information was obtained through Blast2go (v.2.5.0).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Comparative genome analyses and phylogenetics</h3><p>The genomes of <i>M. viride</i> and <i>C. atmophyticus</i> were compared to those of nine other algae, namely <i>Cyanophora paradoxa</i>, <i>Chondrus crispus</i>, <i>C. merolae</i>, <i>M. commoda</i>, <i>Ostreococcus tauri</i>, <i>Chlorella variabilis</i>, <i>Volvox carteri</i> and <i>Chlamydomonas reinhardtii</i>, with the Streptophyta <i>C. braunii, K. nitens, Physcomitrella patens, Selaginella moellendorffii, Oryza sativa</i> subsp. <i>Japonica</i> and <i>Arabidopsis thaliana</i>. The same species were used to define orthogroups (using OrthoFinder, v.1.1.8). Single-copy gene families (that is, gene families with only one gene member per species) were used to construct phylogenetic trees based on maximum likelihood. We first performed multiple sequence alignment by MAFFT (v.7.310) for each single-copy gene orthogroup, followed by gap position removal (only positions where 50% or more of the sequences have a gap are treated as a gap position). A maximum-likelihood phylogenetic tree was constructed for each single-copy orthogroup. Next, we used ASTRAL to combine all single-copy gene trees to a species tree with the multi-species coalescent model. The online tool iTOL was performed to edit and display the final phylogenetic tree.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec17">Gene identification</h3><p>We used various search methods to identify different genes.</p><p>For TFs and TR we used the HMMER search method. We downloaded the HMMER model of the domain structure of each TF from the Pfam website (<a href="https://pfam.xfam.org/">https://pfam.xfam.org/</a>), referring to the TAPscan (v.2) TF database (<a href="https://plantcode.online.uni-marburg.de/tapscan/">https://plantcode.online.uni-marburg.de/tapscan/</a>). Preliminary candidates were collected by searching the HMM profile for each species (&lt;1 × 10<sup>–10</sup>). Then, we filtered genes that did not match the SwissProt functional annotation (&lt;1 × 10<sup>–5</sup>). Finally, we filtered genes containing an incorrect domain according to the domain rules of the TAPscan (v.2) TF database. Most TFs/TRs were confirmed by phylogenetic analysis.</p><p>The HMMER search method was also used for phytohormone signalling pathways. We collected ~10–20 query genes from representative model organisms (for example, <i>A. thaliana</i> and <i>C. reinhardtii</i>). A custom profile HMM was built (hmmer-3.1b2) based on the query genes for each phytohormone. All signalling pathway genes must pass the following two restrictions: (1) genes should pass the HMM profile built by ~10–20 known genes (&lt;1 × 10<sup>–10</sup>), and (2) genes should match the SwissProt functional annotation (&lt;1 × 10<sup>–5</sup>). The custom profile HMM is available in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#MOESM1">Supplementary Information</a> (HMM profiles).</p><p>The genes involved in other pathways, such as biosynthetic phytohormone pathways and cell wall-related genes, were selected based on two criteria : (1) BLAST by known query genes (&lt;1 × 10<sup>–5</sup>), and (2) matching based on SwissProt functional annotation (&lt;1 × 10<sup>–5</sup>).</p><p>For CAZyme annotation we used the dbCAN2 metaserver (<a href="http://bcb.unl.edu/dbCAN2/index.php">http://bcb.unl.edu/dbCAN2/index.php</a>). This server integrates three tools/databases for automated CAZyme annotation: (1) HMMER for annotation of the CAZyme domain against the dbCAN CAZyme domain HMM database; (2) DIAMOND for fast BLAST hits in the CAZy database; and (3) Hotpep for short conserved motifs in the PPR library.</p><p>We also constructed phylogenetic trees to classify certain highly similar genes, including ARF, CSL, HD-ZIP, YUCCA, PIF, TGA and others (see Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#MOESM1">6</a>–<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#MOESM1">13</a>). For phylogenetic analysis of individual genes, we first performed multiple sequence alignment by MAFFT (v.7.310) for each single-copy gene orthogroup, followed by removal of gap position (only positions where 50% or more of the sequences have a gap are treated as gap positions). Then, a maximum-likelihood phylogenetic tree was constructed by RAxML (amino acid substitution model: CAT + GTR, with 500 bootstrap replicates).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec18">Analysis of significantly increased/decreased gene numbers of gene families</h3><p>We used a median gene number to estimate the changes in gene family size<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Hori, K. et al. Klebsormidium flaccidum genome reveals primary factors for plant terrestrial adaptation. Nat. Commun. 5, 3978 (2014)." href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d57113e2723">6</a></sup>, shown in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#Fig1">1d</a>. These showed the gene families whose numbers of genes were significantly increased in embryophytes compared to algae, by calculating the median of the embryophyte gene number/median of the algal gene number ≥10. Taking Rhodophyta and Streptophyta as an example, we selected five representative species for each lineage. Gene numbers of these lineages in the gene family were sorted from largest to smallest. If the median of the Streptophyta gene number/median of the Rhodophyta gene number was &gt;5, this gene family was considered a significantly increased gene family in Streptophyta. The same method is used to define the embryophytes and algal genes in Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#Fig1">1e</a>. If the median gene number of both embryophytes and algae was &gt;0, we defined the genes in this family as those commonly shared between embryophytes and algae. If the median embryophyte gene number was &gt;0 and the median algae gene number was 0, we defined the genes in this family as embryophyte genes. In special cases, if both the median embryophyte and median algae gene number was 0, we removed those low-frequency gene families.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec19">Conserved motif identification</h3><p>The local multiple Em (expectation maximization) for motif elicitation (MEME, <a href="http://meme-suite.org/">http://meme-suite.org/</a>) tool was used to identify conserved motifs. All genes in this study were analysed using the classical model. According to <i>e</i> values, the number of motifs that MEME should find was set to 20.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec20">Reporting Summary</h3><p>Further information on research design is available in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3#MOESM2">Nature Research Reporting Summary</a> linked to this article.</p></div></div></section>
                

                <section aria-labelledby="data-availability" data-title="Data availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="data-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="data-availability">Data availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="data-availability-content">
              
              <p>The whole-genome assemblies and transcriptome for <i>M. viride</i> and <i>C. atmophyticus</i> in this study are deposited at DDBJ/ENA/GenBank under accession nos. <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/gquery/?term=RHPH00000000">RHPH00000000</a> and <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/gquery/?term=RHPI00000000">RHPI00000000</a>. Those data are also available in the CNGB Nucleotide Sequence Archive (accession no. <a href="https://db.cngb.org/search/?q=+CNP0000228">CNP0000228</a>).</p>

    <a data-track="click" data-track-action="outbound reference" data-track-label="link" aria-label="Search for reference 9 on Google Scholar" href="http://scholar.google.com/scholar_lookup?&amp;title=Embryophyte%20stress%20signaling%20evolved%20in%20the%20algal%20progenitors%20of%20land%20plants&amp;journal=Proc.%20Natl%20Acad.%20Sci.%20USA&amp;volume=115&amp;pages=E3471-E3480&amp;publication_year=2018&amp;author=Vries%2CJ&amp;author=Curtis%2CBA&amp;author=Gould%2CSB&amp;author=Archibald%2CJM">

js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec21">Extended data</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec21-content"><div data-test="supplementary-info"><div id="figshareContainer" class="c-article-figshare-container" data-test="figshare-container"></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig6"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3/figures/6" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/esm/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-019-0560-3/MediaObjects/41477_2019_560_Fig6_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Fig. 1 The KEGG distribution of unique proteins in M. viride (blue) and C. atmophyticus (pink).</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>The x-axis indicates the number of genes in a specific category in the respective species. The metabolism pathway is shown on the y-axis.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig7"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3/figures/7" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/esm/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-019-0560-3/MediaObjects/41477_2019_560_Fig7_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Fig. 2 Sequence conservation of various phytohormone receptor genes in representative species.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>Each cell shows a pairwise sequence alignment between a known Arabidopsis protein receptor (top) and the best BLAST hit (E-value &lt;1e−10) in the translated genome of the indicated species. Black and grey represent similar amino acids (the darker the bars, the higher is the similarity).</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig8"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3/figures/8" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/esm/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-019-0560-3/MediaObjects/41477_2019_560_Fig8_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Fig. 3 Analysis of the conserved flagellar proteome in flagellate and non-flagellate organisms and the distribution of key flagellar proteins.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>Key structure-related flagellar proteins in flagellate and non-flagellate algal species in different lineages. The phylogenetic tree on the left panel was constructed using maximum-likelihood method based on the concatenated sequences of single-copy genes from these genomes, after excluding the species-specific gene duplications. The presence (filled circle) or absence (empty circle) of putative orthologs to conserved flagellar proteins is shown on right panel [Based on Reciprocal Blast Hit (RBH) method with Cut-off value of e-5]. The histogram on the lower panel shows the differential expression level of these important structure-related flagellar proteins.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig9"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3/figures/9" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/esm/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-019-0560-3/MediaObjects/41477_2019_560_Fig9_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Fig. 4 Distribution of EF-1α and EF-like motifs.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>Maximum Likelihood was used to infer the phylogenetic tree of the EF-1α and EF-like homologs to understand their phylogenetic distribution. The right panel displays the representative EF-1α and EF-like motifs from red algae to embryophytes. The tree derived from a MAFFT alignment and constructed using IQ-TREE (see Methods). Bootstrap values (500 replicates) ≥50% are shown.</p></div></div><div class="c-article-supplementary__item js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="supp-item" id="Fig10"><h3 class="c-article-supplementary__title u-h3"><a class="print-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view supplementary info" data-track-label="link" data-test="supp-info-link" href="/articles/s41477-019-0560-3/figures/10" data-supp-info-image="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/esm/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41477-019-0560-3/MediaObjects/41477_2019_560_Fig10_ESM.jpg">Extended Data Fig. 5 Phylogenetic tree of phytochrome.</a></h3><div class="c-article-supplementary__description" data-component="thumbnail-container"><p>Maximum Likelihood was used to infer the phylogenetic tree of the phytochrome. The tree derived from a MAFFT alignment and constructed using RAxML (see Methods). Bootstrap values (500 replicates) ≥50% are shown.</p></div></div></div></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec22" data-title="Supplementary information"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec22-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec22">Supplementary information</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec22-content"><div data-test="supplementary-info">
